<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15178000748524481292">In the years before World War I, Byron Aldridge led a charmed life as the charismatic heir apparent to a Pennsylvania timber empire; and to his younger brother, Randolph, he was both guide and idol. But he returned from France a different man and was not home long before those festering memories sent him drifting from one settlement to another, working as a lawman, and the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15178000748524481292">In the years before World War I, Byron Aldridge led a charmed life as the charismatic heir apparent to a Pennsylvania timber empire; and to his younger brother, Randolph, he was both guide and idol. But he returned from France a different man and was not home long before those festering memories sent him drifting from one settlement to another, working as a lawman, and then disappearing altogether.<br /><br />Finally his family discovers him in a remote Louisiana mill town, promptly buys the property, and puts Randolph in charge of this place unlike any he has ever seen, where men are surrounded by cypress swamps and menace, leading lives of ceaseless, backbreaking toil punctuated only by the brutal entertainments provided by the Sicilians who control the whiskey and card games and girls, and by the rough justice meted out by the still-tormented Byron. Randolph struggles to understand him, and to regain his trust, even as their wives presently contend with their own hopes and disappointments and while the future grows uncertain yet fearsome all around them.<br /><br />This is a story about family, about marriage, about what sustains people through loss; it is a reckoning of the sacrifices they must make in order to establish a community in the deepest wilderness, and to defend what is most precious to them. Palpably atmospheric, with a remarkable range of characters and emotions, <strong>The Clearing </strong>displays more powerfully than ever before Tim Gautreaux’s masterful understanding of time, place, and human nature.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15178000748524481292">...more</a>

      </div>

      $('span_class_actionlink_filter_span_tip_102').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]&gt;
</script>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_843681900">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/843681900" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes" class="left imgcol" title="Diane Barnes"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" alt="Diane Barnes" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/843681900?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 11, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes" class="user" title="Diane Barnes">Diane Barnes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=world-war-one" class="actionLinkLite">world-war-one</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer843681900">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17881331219435124340">I am finding it hard to write a review that will do justice to this novel without resorting to over-the-top adjectives and lots of exclamation marks. Let me start with the beautiful language and metaphors that the author uses. Like "the many fanged geography" of the swamp, for one.<br /><br />I guess the best description of this story is multi-layered. There are layers upon layers of history, or personalities, of relationships, of love and hate and justice and personal responsibility. The way that all of th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17881331219435124340">I am finding it hard to write a review that will do justice to this novel without resorting to over-the-top adjectives and lots of exclamation marks. Let me start with the beautiful language and metaphors that the author uses. Like "the many fanged geography" of the swamp, for one.<br /><br />I guess the best description of this story is multi-layered. There are layers upon layers of history, or personalities, of relationships, of love and hate and justice and personal responsibility. The way that all of these things come together to make or destroy human lives is riveting and soul stirring. The characters and setting are so well portrayed that you literally feel like you live with them. And let's not forget the swamp itself, the "many fanged geography" mentioned above. It was a harsh environment that created an isolated existance for the owners and their wives and the sawmill hands. The setting is 1923-1928, and it was quite thought provoking when phone lines were finally installed at the mill and the sherriff was in awe of how easy it was to get in touch with anyone at any time, just because of lines on poles that could find them, just like magic.<br /><br />WWI was over when this story opens, but its effects were still visible in 2 characters in particular,one of the good guys, and one of the bad. A major theme in this book is the morality of killing, even when it's justified, as in war or self defense. "All the things that never happened, just because someone had died."<br /><br />So, as I said, multi-layered. A lot of things, people from different parts of the U.S., from different countries, different ages and philosophies, good and evil, nature, progress, all these things come together to make one hell of a novel from an author I was not familiar with until now. Tim Gautreaux is now on my radar, and I can't wait for a new one from him.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17881331219435124340">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_848870042">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/848870042" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="left imgcol" title="Larry Bassett"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1390874855p2/3816725.jpg" alt="Larry Bassett" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/848870042?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 10, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="user" title="Larry Bassett">Larry Bassett</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=war" class="actionLinkLite">war</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=southern-writers" class="actionLinkLite">southern-writers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer848870042">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10236979629437627735">This is a fascinating story of a lumber mill in Nimbus, Louisiana from the time it sits itself down in the midst of a Cyprus forest in 1923 until six years later with the felling of the Last Tree when it takes itself apart leaving a blind horse and a wasteland of Cyprus stumps. At the end of the story the principal characters, brothers Byron and Randolph, energetically ("grimacing or grinning, who could tell") pump a railroad handcar away from the cleared mill site “toward what they would have a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10236979629437627735">This is a fascinating story of a lumber mill in Nimbus, Louisiana from the time it sits itself down in the midst of a Cyprus forest in 1923 until six years later with the felling of the Last Tree when it takes itself apart leaving a blind horse and a wasteland of Cyprus stumps. At the end of the story the principal characters, brothers Byron and Randolph, energetically ("grimacing or grinning, who could tell") pump a railroad handcar away from the cleared mill site “toward what they would have and what would have them.” <br /><br />But when I got to page 98, I knew one of the things the book was about: war is a business, a deadly business.<br /><blockquote> He’d gone over in 1914 as an observer for the Zeus Powder Company, which paid him to study ammunition consumption so they could plan their factory expansion and production lines. After traveling in France for two months and watching Germany grind Belgium into meal, he wrote home that the U.S. government, nervous about the expanding, ceaseless slaughter, had hired him to provide intelligence. </blockquote><br />I went to enter a new category for <em>The Clearing</em> on my GR page: war. This is a book about war and what it does to men, ordinary men. It shows the true meaning of cannon fodder.<br /><br /><em>The Clearing</em> has something special: good writing. Sometimes there is just a paragraph like this one that does not add a lot to the storyline but is just a joy to read. Merville is the marshal in the town near the lumber mill. In his old age his double barreled shotgun has gotten too heavy for him to carry on his rounds so he takes a small pistol.<br /><blockquote> Merville was in his waterfront office putting down pans over the warped floor boards while a thunderstorm spun whorls of water against the sweating windows. His arthritis bound him at the hips and knees, and his chest ached as if a mule had kicked him. Sitting at his desk, he signed the last form he had to fill out for that night. He was trapped by the storm, immobilized into thinking, and he closed his eyes, remembering his wife, who had hated lightning, and his father, who’d been the same way. Now and then, in the long nights Merville’s life replayed like a wrongly spliced silent film, an overlong saga that always ended with his sitting in this water-stained office, or sometimes in the empty house two blocks away. He looked up at the flickering bulb on its cloth cord, whose light barely revealed the ceiling’s corners where soot-bagged spider webs held leggy husks dead since the war in Cuba. </blockquote><br />This book sometimes just immobilized me into thinking. The pages early in the book describing the slaughter on the WWI battle field come back to me. It’s lasting impact on Byron as he tries to move on with his life. The war that feeds the capitalistic economy and that lays the bodies of young men laden with armaments from the Zeus Powder Company on the barbed wire at the battlefront. When will we ever learn? War is not the answer.<br /><br />War makes men into killers. <em>The Clearing</em> is about men who have learned to be violent killers even in the absence of a war. And (a la the Milgram Shock Experiments of the 1960s) how people will obey orders to behave in a violent manner that they might otherwise decline to do voluntarily.<br /><br />The environmental devastation of the lumber mill – the rape of the land – is equivalent to the moral devastation of warfare. The shell-shocked Byron struggles with a dilemma:<br /><blockquote> “And I will never understand why I was given carte blanche by the United States government to put thirty-caliber slugs into patriotic German kids, when the law, or guilt, or fate wouldn’t let me hunt down and send to hell a one-eyed snake-wielding baby-killer.” </blockquote><br />This is a well-written, powerful book. Having said that, I am torn between four or five stars because things too neatly sort themselves out at the end: a happy ending aided by a lie and unlikely incidents where the bodies nicely compost rather than rot and putrefy. There is too much of a feel-good ending considering all the tragic events of the book. But feel-good with a moral is not bad, a strong four stars. I look forward to reading some of the short stories by <a title="Tim Gautreaux" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/50704.Tim_Gautreaux">Tim Gautreaux</a>. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10236979629437627735">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer47720457">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10705511972169359523">Gautreaux does so many things well, including things I didn't think I'd care about, such as the descriptions of mechanical objects. (I first noticed that in his short story collection.) He has an eloquent, lyrical prose style that creates vivid pictures in your mind as you read. His characters are complex and unique, and you come to care about them. He does interpersonal relationships well, without a hint of sentimentality. The dialogue is a pleasure to read, flavored with just a touch of dialec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10705511972169359523">Gautreaux does so many things well, including things I didn't think I'd care about, such as the descriptions of mechanical objects. (I first noticed that in his short story collection.) He has an eloquent, lyrical prose style that creates vivid pictures in your mind as you read. His characters are complex and unique, and you come to care about them. He does interpersonal relationships well, without a hint of sentimentality. The dialogue is a pleasure to read, flavored with just a touch of dialect to give a sense of where these people are from, and it's a varied cast. <br /><br />I think being from Louisiana helped me enjoy this book, as well as the several themes that the book handles so well; but I was still surprised at how much I enjoyed this story of two brothers running a 1920s sawmill community in a cypress swamp on the north shore of Lake Pontchartrain.<br /><br />Read with a local group for LEH's RELIC program, "Encounter in Louisiana."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10705511972169359523">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_826265694">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/826265694" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/17068912-msj-sarah" class="left imgcol" title="MSJ (Sarah)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445213039p2/17068912.jpg" alt="MSJ (Sarah)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/826265694?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 18, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/17068912-msj-sarah" class="user" title="MSJ (Sarah)">MSJ (Sarah)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to MSJ (Sarah) by: </span>
      On The Southern Literary Trail
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/17068912-msj-sarah?shelf=the-south" class="actionLinkLite">the-south</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/17068912-msj-sarah?shelf=features-a-boat" class="actionLinkLite">features-a-boat</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/17068912-msj-sarah?shelf=great-flood-of-1927" class="actionLinkLite">great-flood-of-1927</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/17068912-msj-sarah?shelf=moonshine" class="actionLinkLite">moonshine</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/17068912-msj-sarah?shelf=war-conflicts" class="actionLinkLite">war-conflicts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer826265694">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9120506521131294298">Magnificent. Tim Gautreaux created a wonderful and compelling tale of two brothers running a saw mill deep in the Louisiana Bayou circa mid-1920's. This is a story about war: between nation-states during the First World War; between the Sicilian mob and the local deputies; and between humans trying to tame the harsh physical environment. But amongst all these conflicts there is deep concern and love towards others, especially between the brothers Randolph and Byron.<br /><br />While reading you can practica</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9120506521131294298">Magnificent. Tim Gautreaux created a wonderful and compelling tale of two brothers running a saw mill deep in the Louisiana Bayou circa mid-1920's. This is a story about war: between nation-states during the First World War; between the Sicilian mob and the local deputies; and between humans trying to tame the harsh physical environment. But amongst all these conflicts there is deep concern and love towards others, especially between the brothers Randolph and Byron.<br /><br />While reading you can practically imagine yourself in the Bayou with the mosquitoes, snakes, alligators, and the thick humidity and heavy rains. Gautreaux really has a knack for creating the physical landscape written on the page. It is its own character.<br /><br />Our collective consciousness often forgets how horrible trench warfare was during WWI and this book brings back those violent and painful images to the forefront of our minds. Shell shock was so very serious for those who returned from the front.<br /><br />Bootleggers, whores, gambling, fist fights and guns. It's all here in all their glory.<br /><br /><strong>"Let me tell you, that Buzetti is one mean son of a bitch. And the people he knows in Chicago will come down here and play marbles with your eyeballs"</strong><br /><br /><em>The Clearing</em> was selected this month by "On The Southern Literary Trail" and I almost passed it over because for some reason the little blurb did not immediately jump out to me. But my library had a copy so I decided to give it a whirl. Big mistake if I skipped this one. A big one.<br /><br />Rating: 5 Stars ("It was amazing"). Will definitely read more from Gautreaux in the near future.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9120506521131294298">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/26906695?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 14, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1213607-julie" class="user" title="Julie">Julie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Julie by: </span>
      Bob
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1213607-julie?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1213607-julie?shelf=usa-historical" class="actionLinkLite">usa-historical</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26906695">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5706696798623503515">A few minutes ago I turned the final page on The Clearing, Tim Gautreaux. I'm still trembling. From general exhaustion and distraction, it took me several days to get through the first 80 or so pages; this morning I went through three cups of coffee and 4 instrumental CDs to read the remaining 225. It's an extraordinary book, a captivating story- I learned something about an era (Prohibition), a place (the swamps of Louisiana), a people (lumber/millworkers) and got caught up with a family that b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5706696798623503515">A few minutes ago I turned the final page on The Clearing, Tim Gautreaux. I'm still trembling. From general exhaustion and distraction, it took me several days to get through the first 80 or so pages; this morning I went through three cups of coffee and 4 instrumental CDs to read the remaining 225. It's an extraordinary book, a captivating story- I learned something about an era (Prohibition), a place (the swamps of Louisiana), a people (lumber/millworkers) and got caught up with a family that both confounded and endeared themselves to me. <br /><br />THere are so many themes here- the devastating effects of war on those who survive it, the hand of the Mob in the depths of the South, rum-runners, brotherly love, desperate violence-both man and nature-made, the hovel-like conditions of a mill settlement, destruction of the South's beautiful forests, the continued degradation of anyone of color, the poor and women. Yet, Gautreaux never preaches, moralizes or paints anything in black and white. The characters are complex, human, flawed and beautiful. It's an outstanding read. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5706696798623503515">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5705879682561023741">This is a strong 4 rating! The author is so detailed with his descriptions of the land and the lumber camp that when the characters leave you feel homesick. It's amazing how detailed he was in describing the climate and weather of the south. Some may think, give it a rest but this detailing made me feel even more connected to the time and place. Many times I found myself scratching my arms just thinking of all the mosquitoes in that southern bayou. Gautreaux does an amazing job with the characte</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5705879682561023741">This is a strong 4 rating! The author is so detailed with his descriptions of the land and the lumber camp that when the characters leave you feel homesick. It's amazing how detailed he was in describing the climate and weather of the south. Some may think, give it a rest but this detailing made me feel even more connected to the time and place. Many times I found myself scratching my arms just thinking of all the mosquitoes in that southern bayou. Gautreaux does an amazing job with the characters. I found that I liked all of "the good guys" even with their faults. This book to me was a story about the clearing of the land and the clearing of the conscience. A very strong sense of family and the healing of the heart and mind. Highly recommend. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1402687322">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1402687322" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/83144-el" class="left imgcol" title="El"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452263504p2/83144.jpg" alt="El" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1402687322?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 30, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83144-el" class="user" title="El">El</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=21st-centurylit" class="actionLinkLite">21st-centurylit</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1402687322">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7494285070959332101">The story seemed fine enough: One brother has returned from France during World War I a very different man (not uncommon, certainly), and he goes off from his Pennsylvania home to do whatever it is changed men do (ie, not settle down). His brother, Randolph, eventually finds him in Louisiana, goes to try to connect with him again, becomes in charge of the mill town, some other stuff happens, SICILIANS, gunfire, some snake drama, talky-talk, and... yeah.<br /><br />It was fine.<br /><br />I expected this to be more like</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7494285070959332101">The story seemed fine enough: One brother has returned from France during World War I a very different man (not uncommon, certainly), and he goes off from his Pennsylvania home to do whatever it is changed men do (ie, not settle down). His brother, Randolph, eventually finds him in Louisiana, goes to try to connect with him again, becomes in charge of the mill town, some other stuff happens, SICILIANS, gunfire, some snake drama, talky-talk, and... yeah.<br /><br />It was fine.<br /><br />I expected this to be more like, I don't know, <a title="Ron Rash" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/24820.Ron_Rash">Ron Rash</a>, or some other American author from the south that people really dig. But this just didn't do it for me. I never felt like I connected with any of the characters, and so therefore had no emotional connection to the story, positive or negative. I felt the writing was uninteresting and just sort of existed on the page, but I missed any sort of real feeling behind the story.<br /><br />I may be disappointed, but I want to try other books by this author, because I think he is someone I would otherwise really enjoy. This one, however, felt like it was trying to be a movie, and therefore didn't work so well as an actual book. Just mentioning a swamp isn't enough to make me feel like I'm actually in Louisiana.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7494285070959332101">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/1977270-meran" class="left imgcol" title="Meran"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1341306725p2/1977270.jpg" alt="Meran" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <a href="/user/show/1977270-meran" class="user" title="Meran">Meran</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/932711.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9781400030538" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1977270-meran?shelf=historical" class="actionLinkLite">historical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1977270-meran?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1977270-meran?shelf=southern-us" class="actionLinkLite">southern-us</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1977270-meran?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1977270-meran?shelf=on-the-southern-literary-trail-grou" class="actionLinkLite">on-the-southern-literary-trail-grou</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer825158727">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2836681109930453306">This is the story of two brothers, one broken in the War in France, the other remained at home helping their father out in his wood mill business.<br /><br />The "whole" one, Randolph, finally finds the "broken" one, Byron, who's been lost to them for 4 yrs, wandering the US, doing work as a law keeper. It's Randolph's duty to bring Byron home.<br /><br />It's through the characters, though, that we're brought to the understanding about the horrors of all Wars, and how the men who return are all damaged, as how could t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2836681109930453306">This is the story of two brothers, one broken in the War in France, the other remained at home helping their father out in his wood mill business.<br /><br />The "whole" one, Randolph, finally finds the "broken" one, Byron, who's been lost to them for 4 yrs, wandering the US, doing work as a law keeper. It's Randolph's duty to bring Byron home.<br /><br />It's through the characters, though, that we're brought to the understanding about the horrors of all Wars, and how the men who return are all damaged, as how could they not be?, but learn to get through the experiences and still somehow live life. (See pgs 99 thru 103.)<br /><br />Besides illustrating the effects of War on men, we see what the times were like for children workers in the mills; how bad the working conditions were for all who lived the experiences; the wounds they took and the sometimes quick, but sometimes messy deaths; the music of the time; the lovely forests of the era, no longer around in ours; the plight of the displaced wildlife. All clear images and sad beyond belief or understanding.<br /><br />The poor old blind horse!!! Left behind to be dinner for an alligator… I wish THAT had been different.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2836681109930453306">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_864411689">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/864411689" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1301039-frankh" class="left imgcol" title="FrankH"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1412017714p2/1301039.jpg" alt="FrankH" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/864411689?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 25, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1301039-frankh" class="user" title="FrankH">FrankH</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer864411689">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9209072763590404451"> <br />Group Read: On the Southern Literary Trail<br /><br />Comments: <br /><br />'The Clearing' by Louisianan Tim Gautreaux has a lot going for it, but it also contains enough niggling disconnects and imbalances in plotting and character to make me wonder if the story itself was meant for the telling. <br /><br />Gautreaux excels with his lush detailing of the steamy, southern Louisiana bayou lumber mill and there are many fine passages of descriptive writing -- the author's precise rendering of the workmen bringing down the final cyp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9209072763590404451"> <br />Group Read: On the Southern Literary Trail<br /><br />Comments: <br /><br />'The Clearing' by Louisianan Tim Gautreaux has a lot going for it, but it also contains enough niggling disconnects and imbalances in plotting and character to make me wonder if the story itself was meant for the telling. <br /><br />Gautreaux excels with his lush detailing of the steamy, southern Louisiana bayou lumber mill and there are many fine passages of descriptive writing -- the author's precise rendering of the workmen bringing down the final cypress in the camp is just one example among many. But, ultimately, the novel collapses under its own weight because the chief protagonists, Byron and Randolph Aldridge, keep dithering and won't or can't come to terms with the core issues in their lives. <br /><br />It's difficult to say what Gautreaux had in mind for the brothers. Fallible characters in a perpetual holding pattern? A study in passivity? Themes of coping and forbearance factor in the human condition, so they're shouldn't be declared off-limits to the enterprising, psychologically oriented novelist brave enough to tackle them. But what obtains when they're allowed to dominate in a long-form shoot-em-up like 'The Clearing'? My thought: That's a dog that won't hunt. <br /><br /><strong>Impasse #1</strong><br /><br />We are meant to understand that part of the Randolph's mission in coming to Louisiana is to bring home or otherwise rehabilitate the prodigal brother Byron, the parish sheriff of mill town Nimbus, still struggling psychologically with what he saw and did as the WWI doughboy fighting the Germans on the killing fields of France. Yet, as Randolph reports to his father on his brother's condition, no plan is forthcoming for getting Byron into whatever constitutes therapy in the 1920s or, for that matter, putting him out of harm's way, back in the family home located in Pittsburgh. Following the end of the war, some bad blood has flowed between the father, Noah, and his son Byron, so reaching reconciliation may prove difficult. All of them probably desire it, yet due to the nature of Byron's intractable, disordered outlook on life, they can't quite determine how to achieve it. The author has put this issue front and center as a story element, but then permits the characters to walk away from it for most of the novel. We're adrift here.<br /><br /><strong>Impasse #2</strong><br /><br />At the outset, Randolph believes his tenure in Louisiana is linked to the time it takes to clear the forest -- three years (it will actually take five years before the last cypress is harvested). But neither he nor constable Byron has a time-line for ending the threat of Sicilian mobster Buzetti and henchman Crouch and for closing down the Nimbus honkeytonk and pimping operation preying upon the workers. It's more a matter of containment than a test of courage: Byron thinks closing the saloon would lead to men quitting the camp for the siren allure -- such as it is -- of nearby Tiger Island. Better to keep the men close, he muses, and let them blow off steam in Nimbus where he can be sure the body count won't get too high. As a result of this thinking, though, we continue to get one violent bar and gambling incident after another, without much material change in the nature of the conflict other than an escalated threat level,temporary at its best, a charade at its worst. <br /><br />At one point, when Byron drags Buzetti's Tiger Creek Saloon into the swamp as a reprisal for a death at Nimbus, the matter appears to be heading towards a final showdown. Buzetti's gunmen come to the camp, looking for vengeance, but then surprisingly pull back as the parish sheriff and the mill hands put on a show of force. The next thing we know Buzetti is approaching Randolph on another matter -- allowing the Sicilian's cousin Vincente to return to his old job as the house gambling operator at the Nimbus saloon. Gautreaux here has doubled down on non-sequiturs. It's strange Buzetti has implicitly dropped the matter of the Tiger Creek Saloon destruction, stranger yet Randolph acts on Buzetti's recommendation to persuade his brother to agree to Vicente's return (Convenient, though, because this turn of events keeps Vicente at Nimbus where he will be killed later by Randolph in another episode). Once again, the Aldridges demonstrate an inability to address an overriding concern in the novel. We've seen the heavy cloud but can't get the rain. <br /><br /><strong>Impasse #3</strong><br /><br />When the story begins, Ralph is writing to his father about the suffering mill hands, characterizing them as 'rougher' than those in other camps and infused with a sense of 'deprivation or old wrong being done that has gone into their bones'. Yet Gautreaux offers no fully-developed character that gives us a window into this psychology. We are simply being asked to accept the idea that, intrinsically, men like this, under these conditions, behave on the weekend like deranged sub-human sailors on shore leave. With a more temperate work force, of course, the Buzetti problem -- and much of the story -- goes away, or at least improves. But the mill manager makes no effort to figure out what drives the men to self-destructive behavior and makes no move to change the hell-bent, whiskey bound culture found in the camp. Rando should be smarter than this. Following an idea from the Sicilians, can someone please drop a few dollars on these benighted workers and <em>incentivize</em> them to boycott the bar? We're bogged down here.<br /><br /><strong>A Change in the Weather</strong><br /><br />So, question is, how does this story move forward? Well, it doesn't because it can't, for some prolonged period of time. Finally the actions of three minor players -- the housekeeper May, Randolph's wife Lillian and the old sheriff Merville Thiboudeaux -- break up the roadblocks and lead to altered circumstances and a final climax. It's no thanks to the Aldridges. <br /><br />I liked the idea that the old Acadian lawman Merville wants to do one good deed before he dies, then steps up with a plan to interdict an illegal bootleg delivery and arrest Buzetti and his men. Still, this represents an abrupt change in point of view. The book goes to great lengths to depict the havoc the Sicilians have visited upon the lumber mill family and, in terms of story logic, they're the ones that need to instrument the action to take down Buzetti, not Thiboudeaux. Deputizing the Aldridges doesn't quite cut it.<br /><br />Lillian's arrival surely changes the weather of the story; she's setting up church meetings for the working men and their wives and planning to school the kids, but it's an idea which should have occurred much earlier to her husband. May makes a different contribution -- producing her infant son Walter who finds a path into the heart of his uncle and putative father Byron and starts his emotional healing that's been beyond the reach of the Aldridges since day one. You can credit this development to the grace of God or, more directly, to May's stealthy bone-jumping prowess but either way it's clear little Walter's benign effect on Byron's mental balance does not proceed organically from any Aldridge agency. <br /><br /><strong> Final Thoughts</strong><br /><br />And then again maybe all this stems from the fact I'm very impatient with deliberating, passive characters who can't take care of business in a timely manner.<br /><br />I certainly understand 'The Clearing' has its champions and I won't discount the possibility I've 'misread' the novel. I surely know Gautreaux can write. When I picked up this volume at the library, I also grabbed his 'Same Place, 'Same Things', an early story collection that I found technically sure-footed and so inventive and incisive I devoured it almost in a single sitting (from where I sit, it definitely warrants a five-star rating). <br /><br />Still, one day I'd like to have a little talk with Gautreaux's editor.<br /><br />Good or Not So Good, ya gotta call 'em like ya see 'em.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9209072763590404451">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_852160052">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/852160052" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3787451-ctgt" class="left imgcol" title="Ctgt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446131389p2/3787451.jpg" alt="Ctgt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/852160052?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 11, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3787451-ctgt" class="user" title="Ctgt">Ctgt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer852160052">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3446046737385030106">This is a period piece set in a remote sawmill town in Louisiana just after WWI. A story about two brothers Byron and Randolph. Older brother Byron is the golden boy heir to the family timber business until he is sent as an observer and then later a combatant in WWI. He returns from overseas a changed man and promptly takes off drifting from town to town as a lawman wanting nothing to do with the family. The domineering father locates the prodigal in the milltown of Nimbus, buys the property and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3446046737385030106">This is a period piece set in a remote sawmill town in Louisiana just after WWI. A story about two brothers Byron and Randolph. Older brother Byron is the golden boy heir to the family timber business until he is sent as an observer and then later a combatant in WWI. He returns from overseas a changed man and promptly takes off drifting from town to town as a lawman wanting nothing to do with the family. The domineering father locates the prodigal in the milltown of Nimbus, buys the property and sends Randolph down to bring his brother back into the family fold.<br /><br />Gautreaux did a wonderful job of transporting me both back in time and right into the middle of a swamp edged cypress stand. It's very easy in this technological day and age to forget exactly how isolated these small towns were back then. Basically a mini fiefdom to be controlled by whoever has the willpower. I could feel the sweat rolling off me as he described the heat and humidity and all the local nasties (snakes, gators, skeeters) that infested the area.<br /><br />Secondary characters were a little thin on development but watching the relationship between the brothers morph throughout the story was the primary and most fascinating focus. A very interesting look at family and the effect of war on the pysche of men.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3446046737385030106">...more</a>

          </span>

          <a href="/book/show/1093284.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340833186" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/14630856-peter?shelf=reviews" class="actionLinkLite">reviews</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer850237823">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2658791256449248741">Set in a Louisiana lumber camp in the 1920s, <em>The Clearing</em> sounded promising but proved otherwise, despite its many positive reviews. I would not be so cynical as to suggest that Tim Gautreaux had his eye on Hollywood, but his novel does read like <em>Clearing – the Book of the Film</em>. It is fiercely incident-driven, progressing by a series of confrontations, stand-offs, fist-fights, razor-fights, and gun-fights, with an occasional explosion and alligator-attack. Good guys and bad guys are firmly demar</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2658791256449248741">Set in a Louisiana lumber camp in the 1920s, <em>The Clearing</em> sounded promising but proved otherwise, despite its many positive reviews. I would not be so cynical as to suggest that Tim Gautreaux had his eye on Hollywood, but his novel does read like <em>Clearing – the Book of the Film</em>. It is fiercely incident-driven, progressing by a series of confrontations, stand-offs, fist-fights, razor-fights, and gun-fights, with an occasional explosion and alligator-attack. Good guys and bad guys are firmly demarcated and everyone is given a back history to explain their actions. Good guys who are surely about to die manage, after much hand-wringing and brow-mopping, to recover. The bad guys don't. The ending you will have seen in any cheap cinema near you. I suppose I was expecting something a bit more than a popular novel , but there’s not much space for the reader or the reader’s imagination in <em>The Clearing</em>. You are just going to get spoonfed a narrative about two brothers in a swamp, with a lot of description but not much atmosphere. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2658791256449248741">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer202765444648913861">The story is so well developed that you find you must read it. You cannot, however, quickly read this without committing to it. The read is so richly detailed that you move along as though you're in the damp humid swamps of the lumber mill.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText202765444648913861">The story is so well developed that you find you must read it. You cannot, however, quickly read this without committing to it. The read is so richly detailed that you move along as though you're in the damp humid swamps of the lumber mill. The characters are vivid and heroic but are real in that they rise to the circumstances they are in without becoming more than human. From the World War I vet battling his demons to his mill managing brother trying to save him, save his mill from the elements that weigh in at every turn and save himself, there is something on every blessed page of this book. The story slowly builds in the beginning, but be ready for it to take off.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer110497699">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10453325547623096877">A high rating, because I could not bear to be too far away from this book over the past few days. It is very much like books I have admired by Robert Goolrick, and most recently, Richard Ford and Larry Watson. They share the qualities of American bewilderment: the ethics of confronting those without conscience, the creation of order where there is only vestigial law, and the inevitable compromises one risks when striving to rescue himself, or others. It is superbly written; one feels the sweat d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10453325547623096877">A high rating, because I could not bear to be too far away from this book over the past few days. It is very much like books I have admired by Robert Goolrick, and most recently, Richard Ford and Larry Watson. They share the qualities of American bewilderment: the ethics of confronting those without conscience, the creation of order where there is only vestigial law, and the inevitable compromises one risks when striving to rescue himself, or others. It is superbly written; one feels the sweat drip and the mules steam. I should mention its semblances to Faulkner separately, because they are among the strongest of its qualities. Gautreaux shares with Faulkner a dry voice and an intimacy with the swamps where most of identitye gets reformed, typically not for the better. It may not appear on many must-read lists, but it should, first as a book that follows a shattered life from the Great War, and second as an example of fiction about shaping and harvesting the cypress of American character. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10453325547623096877">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_15058701">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15058701" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/893669-sabra" class="left imgcol" title="Sabra"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1202699539p2/893669.jpg" alt="Sabra" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/15058701?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/893669-sabra" class="user" title="Sabra">Sabra</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/893669-sabra?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer15058701">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3348548831331438702">Casualties <br /><br />Wars of different sorts are a main theme in The Clearing, by Tim Gautreaux. It amazes me with the artfulness of the language and how the careful details put you in the place. I can easily imagine being knee-deep in Louisiana swamp water keeping an eye out for water moccasins gliding by. <br /><br />Two of the characters, both lawmen, have been through their own wars. The older saw the Civil War destroy his home, his father's dignity, the family's livelihood. The memory of running into the sugar c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3348548831331438702">Casualties <br /><br />Wars of different sorts are a main theme in The Clearing, by Tim Gautreaux. It amazes me with the artfulness of the language and how the careful details put you in the place. I can easily imagine being knee-deep in Louisiana swamp water keeping an eye out for water moccasins gliding by. <br /><br />Two of the characters, both lawmen, have been through their own wars. The older saw the Civil War destroy his home, his father's dignity, the family's livelihood. The memory of running into the sugar cane fields to see a battle on his own land is staggering – arms flying through the air, screams, confusion. The vision, and the sense of loss inflicted on his family, he remembers into his old age.<br /><br />The younger man finally brings himself to talk about what he saw in France during the First World War. Humans vanishing into a red spray. Waves and waves of expendable soldiers piling up on the field at the orders of a general behind the lines. A mortar shell crashed and his friend exploded into parts. Later in the hospital, they dug shrapnel from his back, along with four of the friend's teeth that were embedded in his flesh. <br /><br />There they are in the middle of a Louisiana swamp when a war of a different sort finds them and changes everyone concerned ever after.<br /><br />For days now, I've been waterlogged by the descriptions of the humidity, the heat, the sweat. I've flinched at the razor blades pulled in bar fights. I've felt like I'm peering into the heavy darkness with a pitiful flashlight looking for the gator that pulled off one of the workers. I've wondered if the anguished minds of the battle scarred will ever heal, even though it's certain they never can forget what they've seen. It's a book that transports you to another place. Even though I finished it last night, there's still a little bit of me lost in that bayou.<br />How odd that we count casualties only in terms of the dead; when the injured will never have the same life, and the very act of witness means you are forever changed from who you were. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3348548831331438702">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/858967-dera-weaver" class="left imgcol" title="Dera Weaver"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Dera Weaver" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/65859909?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 02, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/858967-dera-weaver" class="user" title="Dera Weaver">Dera Weaver</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65859909">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10235858283028391285">I fell in love with Gautreaux after reading one of his short stories in the New Yorker--he's got that Southern thing down pat, the water and the green dark and the ruin and the snakes and the violence. I decided to read everything he'd written. Now I'm not as sure as I was: I love the writing, but I'm thinking he may be better for the short-story genre. Characters are a little boiler-plated, I fear, and the plot never just wound me up and dragged me along. Still, I do love the writing.<br /> The story</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10235858283028391285">I fell in love with Gautreaux after reading one of his short stories in the New Yorker--he's got that Southern thing down pat, the water and the green dark and the ruin and the snakes and the violence. I decided to read everything he'd written. Now I'm not as sure as I was: I love the writing, but I'm thinking he may be better for the short-story genre. Characters are a little boiler-plated, I fear, and the plot never just wound me up and dragged me along. Still, I do love the writing.<br /> The story turns around two brothers, lumber mill owners in the mid-1920's, and the setting is a Louisiana cypress swamp that's being taken out, systematically and with an eye on the (highly profitable) end of the endeavor. There's the expected multi-layered conflict between the two brothers, a sinister threat from outside that pulls them together, and there are lots and lots of saloon fights. Lots of them. <br /> Gautreaux doesn't sentimentalize the destruction of the old-growth trees, but he doesn't let us look away from it, either. I found myself drawn to the details of lumbering more than to the human story, maybe because I think I DO sentimentalize the loss of great forests.<br /> So I'll probably still go on and read all of Gautreaux's work, but I'm not quite as much in love as I was before this one.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10235858283028391285">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_536498494">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/536498494" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9588678-nick-jones" class="left imgcol" title="Nick Jones"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Nick Jones" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/536498494?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 26, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9588678-nick-jones" class="user" title="Nick Jones">Nick Jones</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/932711.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9781400030538" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer536498494">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4806845239830184074">This is a page-turner, a good read. It has a story that rampages forward and Tim Gautreaux has a turn of phrase that signifies literary quality. Yet it took me about three months to read it. I would constantly put it down and then have no inclination to pick it up again, instead finding something else to read. There are certain books that make me think they would make a good film: they have a clear story – or, more importantly, a story that breaks into dramatic scenes; vivid characters who are n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4806845239830184074">This is a page-turner, a good read. It has a story that rampages forward and Tim Gautreaux has a turn of phrase that signifies literary quality. Yet it took me about three months to read it. I would constantly put it down and then have no inclination to pick it up again, instead finding something else to read. There are certain books that make me think they would make a good film: they have a clear story – or, more importantly, a story that breaks into dramatic scenes; vivid characters who are not burdened by intricate inner lives or complex motivations: they tend to be readable but not necessarily good books. At first I thought The Clearing would make a good film, but after a while I began to think it was a film...or, rather, that its imaginative life came from the cinema. I don’t want to sound snotty about cinema: in a film a seemingly stereotyped or conventional character can be a complex play between generic expectations, star persona and performance, but in The Clearing, while the characters pretended to be ‘real’, they were little more than filmic types: they were mannequins pretending to be people. Everything they did followed the conventions of movies. And this, presumably, is the reason it is so readable: we already know everything about the characters, we know what they will do, we can follow them without surprise or thought, we don’t have to wonder about their motivations, we are free to rush forward and consume event after event without any real thought and only the simplest and most hackneyed emotional response. No, I didn’t like this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4806845239830184074">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer52801774">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14963318464480530811"> Set in a cedar forest in post-WW I Louisiana, this book follows the fortunes of two brothers, sons of a lumber magnate, one of whom has returned burned out from the horrors of Europe in WW I and is working as a deputy in the sawmill village, and the other who has come down from Pittsburgh to run the operation. We watch as they battle badmouths, cottonmouths, and a depression-era version of organized crime. Blood is spilled, lessons are learned, and redemption is found. I really liked this book</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14963318464480530811"> Set in a cedar forest in post-WW I Louisiana, this book follows the fortunes of two brothers, sons of a lumber magnate, one of whom has returned burned out from the horrors of Europe in WW I and is working as a deputy in the sawmill village, and the other who has come down from Pittsburgh to run the operation. We watch as they battle badmouths, cottonmouths, and a depression-era version of organized crime. Blood is spilled, lessons are learned, and redemption is found. I really liked this book for its hard-nosed evocation of logging and milling under totally obnoxious conditions. I wasn't always persuaded by the characters, but it's a great page-turning story. I became interested in this because Mr. Gautreaux has a new book, The Missing, the review of which referred favorably to The Clearing. So, now, happily on to his new work. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12145149119408587396">The title has multiple meanings: a clearing of the conscience, a clearing of guilt, a clearing of the mind, and of course, a clearing of the land. There is less ecological emphasis in this book than in Serena; instead it focuses on a drama of violence and retribution, reminding me of a kind of Clint Eastwood movie, either The Man With No Name western series or Gran Toreno (however it's spelled). It is the story of two brothers who act as foils to one another at the beginning but gradually come t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12145149119408587396">The title has multiple meanings: a clearing of the conscience, a clearing of guilt, a clearing of the mind, and of course, a clearing of the land. There is less ecological emphasis in this book than in Serena; instead it focuses on a drama of violence and retribution, reminding me of a kind of Clint Eastwood movie, either The Man With No Name western series or Gran Toreno (however it's spelled). It is the story of two brothers who act as foils to one another at the beginning but gradually come together at the end. Once again, the setting influences much of the raw action of the plot. What is it about these Southern writers and their settings? It is almost like reading Thomas Hardy again. I'm looking forward to taking on The Missing by the same author soon.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12709236623986102355">When I worked in a bookstore one day I took a phone call from a publisher who wanted to recommend we take a look at a couple of books, one of them being Tim Gautreaux's The Clearing. I found a copy of the book and read it. It was an amazing piece of work. A quick review of the story didn't capture my attention but I began to read it and was totally sold on the writing.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText12709236623986102355">When I worked in a bookstore one day I took a phone call from a publisher who wanted to recommend we take a look at a couple of books, one of them being Tim Gautreaux's The Clearing. I found a copy of the book and read it. It was an amazing piece of work. A quick review of the story didn't capture my attention but I began to read it and was totally sold on the writing. When The Missing was released, I immediately got a copy and was once again captivated by Gautreaux's writing. The story this time around was a bit more interesting so the total package of The Missing is a highly recommended work. Gautreaux has a great sense of place, is a good story teller, and writes with great skill. </span>

He is currently one of my favourite authors along with Glenn Taylor, Cormac McCarthy and Charles Portis. Happy Reading!</span>

He writes wonderfully descriptive stories of the South. Hope this is as good.<br /><br />From a review: "You might learn more about old-time logging than you ever wanted to know, but the story is as compelling as Cold Mountain or All the Pretty Horses and just as well written. "<br /><br />Loved this. I learned a lot about logging in the old primal forests that were too quickly gone. I've started Last Night at Twisted River and both delve into logging at opposite ends (north and south) of the U.S. He writes great stories and characters you can care about. Can't wait to start another one.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15266774220156174438">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1384405846">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1384405846" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/25756716-the-cannibal" class="left imgcol" title="The Cannibal"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1384682914p2/25756716.jpg" alt="The Cannibal" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1384405846?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25756716-the-cannibal" class="user" title="The Cannibal">The Cannibal</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18721223-le-dernier-arbre" title="Hardcover 9782021082739" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1384405846">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9213210861993915439"> Bienvenue à Nimbus, concession forestière des Aldridge, un des nombreux trous du cul de la Louisiane. Bienvenue dans un enfer chaud et humide.<br /><br />Attention, regardez où vous mettez les pieds car il y a des mocassins d’eau qui se nichent dans les flaques boueuses. Et rien à voir avec la chanson  tes mocassins et les miens  car ici, nous parlons de serpents d’eau.<br /><br />Dans ce roman, les gars, va falloir bosser dur durant de longues années, le temps de couper tous les arbres, des cyprès chauves. Scier les</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9213210861993915439"> Bienvenue à Nimbus, concession forestière des Aldridge, un des nombreux trous du cul de la Louisiane. Bienvenue dans un enfer chaud et humide.<br /><br />Attention, regardez où vous mettez les pieds car il y a des mocassins d’eau qui se nichent dans les flaques boueuses. Et rien à voir avec la chanson ? tes mocassins et les miens ? car ici, nous parlons de serpents d’eau.<br /><br />Dans ce roman, les gars, va falloir bosser dur durant de longues années, le temps de couper tous les arbres, des cyprès chauves. Scier les troncs, les débiter 6 jours sur 7 avant de vous saouler la gueule du samedi soir au lundi matin dans le bastringue tenu par un sicilien louche ayant des cousins mafiosi.<br /><br />Oubliez les syndicats et les droits des travailleurs, car en 1923, seuls les riches ont des droits. Je ne vous parlerai même pas du cas où vous seriez de couleur… là, le mot "droit" n’existe même pas pour vous, hormis celui de fermer votre gueule.<br /><br />Sans user d’artifices, l’auteur nous décrit l’Amérique des années 20, celle qui avance à pas de géant, qui industrialise tout, qui déforeste tout… Le roman vous plongera dans un marais où les conditions de vie et de travail sont inhumaines, les accidents graves ou mortels nombreux et où le racisme, tel l’alligator dans le bayou, règne en maître.<br /><br />Utilisant une multitude de personnages, tous bien travaillés, tous bien distincts – certains étant même très attachants – l’auteur explore une partie des années 20, avec tout ce qu’elles avaient de démesuré niveau progrès industriel (le téléphone et les constructions à tout va, en bois). Sans oublier le traumatisme de la Première Guerre, bien présent chez un des frères Aldridge, Byron.<br /><br />C’est toute la vie de la concession forestière qui se déroule dans ce roman aussi profond que l’étendue des cyprès : les maladies, les accidents, le débit de boisson, la mafia qui tient les ouvriers par l’alcool, les putes et le jeu, ces hommes dépensant jusqu’à leur dernier sous dans ce bouge dégoutant.<br /><br />La sueur a coulée sur mon front durant la lecture, non pas que le roman était pompant, mais il est tellement puissant que j’ai été emportée dans le bayou, suivant ses méandres tortueux et boueux, j’ai pataugé dans tout cela et j’en suis ressortie bouleversée, épuisée, secouée… L’âme de certains hommes est plus boueuse et tortueuse que les méandres de ce diable de bayou !<br /><br />La tension est palpable tout au long de l’histoire, les salauds vous harcèlent comme un moustique la nuit, vous ne savez jamais quand ils vont frapper et c’est au moment où l’attention se relâche qu’ils en profiteront pour vous piquer définitivement d’une balle bien placée.<br /><br />Il y a aussi dans ce récit de l’amour fraternel, celui d’un frère cadet (Randolph) qui ne sait rien de la Grande Guerre et qui voudrait aider son aîné (Byron) à se ressaisir, lui qui a vu les horreurs de Verdun. Un père aussi, qui voulait que son fil Byron fasse la guerre, qu’il soit un héros, qu’il aille au feu et qui ne comprend pas pourquoi il est revenu traumatisé, se réfugiant dans l’alcool et le fuyant comme la peste.<br /><br />Tant de personnages dont j’ai partagé la vie, les souvenirs sur cette Amérique, tout ces gens que je dois quitter, maintenant, les laisser aller ailleurs, détruire une autre forêt (pour les Aldridge), reconstruire d’autres baraquements minables et continuer à se faire exploiter car une partie de ces pauvres gars, après 5 années de dur labeur, n’ont même pas mis un dollar de côté sur un compte en banque et repartent avec les mêmes frusques sur le dos.<br /><br />Un grand roman coup de cœur qui ne vous laissera pas indifférent, un roman sur l’impitoyable capitalisme des années 20 dans une Amérique qui se gave de progrès technique au point de ne pas réfléchir et de détruire tout le capital « arbres ». Ici, c’est pas Zorro qui fait la loi, mais les Zéro qu’on a après les chiffres, sur un compte en banque.<br /><br />Un grand roman presque en vase-clos, dans un décor dévasté par les crues, la boue… Une nature qui était magnifique mais qui sera dévastée car ici, on coupe les arbres jusqu’au dernier…<br /><br />Enfile tes bottes, prends ta scie et regarde où tu marches, des fois qu’un alligator ou un serpent te mordrais… gaffe aussi aux mafiosi, comme ces animaux précités, ils n’aiment pas qu’on les dérange dans leur petit business…<br /><br />Prends une Winchester à plusieurs coups et laisse-toi tenter par un verre de whisky frelaté dans le troquet. La paye n’est pas bonne, la boisson non plus, les conditions de travail sont merdiques, mais putain, tu vas lire un sacré bon roman !<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9213210861993915439">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_935553480">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/935553480" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2104041-dayne" class="left imgcol" title="Dayne"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442472280p2/2104041.jpg" alt="Dayne" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/935553480?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 16, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2104041-dayne" class="user" title="Dayne">Dayne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer935553480">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5386681256046565382">This novel takes place in the southwest Louisiana of the 1920s, a place at the very end of the virgin cypress era, a region no more tame than a sack plumb full of cottonmouth moccasins. In this tale, Tim Gautreaux's fourth book-length work of fiction, all of the innate wildness and danger of the cypress swamp comes full bore, like a reader's feast of unforgettable words.<br /><br />Two brothers, Byron and Randolph Aldridge, the sons of a Pennsylvania timber baron, go south for different reasons. Byron, a la</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5386681256046565382">This novel takes place in the southwest Louisiana of the 1920s, a place at the very end of the virgin cypress era, a region no more tame than a sack plumb full of cottonmouth moccasins. In this tale, Tim Gautreaux's fourth book-length work of fiction, all of the innate wildness and danger of the cypress swamp comes full bore, like a reader's feast of unforgettable words.<br /><br />Two brothers, Byron and Randolph Aldridge, the sons of a Pennsylvania timber baron, go south for different reasons. Byron, a lawman in the fictional Nimbus, is fleeing his memory and his war-torn past, the skull-wrenching effects of World War I service. His younger brother attempts to bring Byron back to the family and to sanity. This attempt, coupled with the hazards of the swamp, crazed sawmill workers, corrupt marshals, and the liquor-running mob, is slowly fashioned into a beautiful piece of work.<br /><br />I will only mention Cormac McCarthy (All the Pretty Horses) in passing. Not mentioning McCarthy would be a mistake, for this book has the beautiful marks of McCarthy's influence on Gautreaux, and the language used throughout the story is at a level on par with McCarthy's inestimable prose. Ultimately, this is a novel about love and lust, place and period that only Gautreaux could write, now at the height of his powers as one of the South's greatest living authors.<br /><br />The Clearing had me involved in a cast of characters far beyond what I'm used to. Believe it or not, I read it on my honeymoon. This says less about me and more about the strength of Gautreaux's novel.<br /><br />A marvelous Louisiana story for our times. I highly recommend this novel.<br /><br />--Dayne Sherman, Welcome to the Fallen Paradise: A Novel and Zion: A Novel (coming soon)</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11229116102639182107">The Clearing is not a book to go to if you're looking for fast-paced action or a driving narrative.
