<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1477137422">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1477137422" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5303348-kevin-farrell" class="left imgcol" title="Kevin Farrell"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1303478257p2/5303348.jpg" alt="Kevin Farrell" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1477137422?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 23, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5303348-kevin-farrell" class="user" title="Kevin Farrell">Kevin Farrell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1477137422">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1011267970187666236">I recently (strongly) recommended this book to a good friend of mine. I told him that I think Tim Gautreaux does for Tennessee what Mark Twain did for Missouri. He makes it come alive in your hands.<br /><br />I can not agree with those who rate this book lower than I, but can only assume that they might be put off by the violent living conditions and people in this story of a southern timber operation. This author doesn't even know what political correctness is, and I am pleased. Another issue that some re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1011267970187666236">I recently (strongly) recommended this book to a good friend of mine. I told him that I think Tim Gautreaux does for Tennessee what Mark Twain did for Missouri. He makes it come alive in your hands.<br /><br />I can not agree with those who rate this book lower than I, but can only assume that they might be put off by the violent living conditions and people in this story of a southern timber operation. This author doesn't even know what political correctness is, and I am pleased. Another issue that some readers may have is the (accurate) 1920s description of southern black workers. They are free men but still mostly treated as property by white employers.<br /><br />If you have a weak stomach and abhor accurate descriptions of violence, you should avoid this one. The story is a great, wild ride into a world that most of us never see or hear about. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1011267970187666236">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_900521135">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/900521135" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1876065-mike" class="left imgcol" title="Mike"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1277896615p2/1876065.jpg" alt="Mike" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/900521135?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 04, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1876065-mike" class="user" title="Mike">Mike</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153786.The_Clearing" title="Paperback 9780340828908" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1876065-mike?shelf=fiction-historical" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-historical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1876065-mike?shelf=fiction-american" class="actionLinkLite">fiction-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer900521135">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5889770432650150977">Quite a gripping read. A mix of almost 'wild west' (though set in the deep south), environmental and anti-war novel. An original take on early 20th century America, seeing it from the point of view of two brothers whose business it is to despoil the cypress forests of Louisiana - awareness of the destruction they are inflicting is only just edging into their consciousness. The setting is a lawless isolated logging town. One brother is crippled by what we now know as PTSD, from his war experience</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5889770432650150977">Quite a gripping read. A mix of almost 'wild west' (though set in the deep south), environmental and anti-war novel. An original take on early 20th century America, seeing it from the point of view of two brothers whose business it is to despoil the cypress forests of Louisiana - awareness of the destruction they are inflicting is only just edging into their consciousness. The setting is a lawless isolated logging town. One brother is crippled by what we now know as PTSD, from his war experiences. The other is trying to come out from the shadow of his older brother, and from the control of his father.<br /><br />There a few cliches here - the younger brother being saved from drowning under ice by his older brother; the violent climax which could be from almost any western film.<br /><br />But Gautreux writes well, and the story is absorbing.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5889770432650150977">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  }

The hooded members wage a bloody year-long campaign of terror that culminates in a massacre where the innocent suffer alongside the guilty. Caught in the maelstrom of the Mitcham war are four people: the aging sheriff sympathetic to both sides; the widowed midwife who delivered nearly every member of Hell-at-the-Breech; a ruthless detective who wages his own war against the gang; and a young store clerk who harbors a terrible secret. Based on incidents that occurred a few miles from the author\'s childhood home, Hell at the Breech chronicles the events of dark days that led the people involved to discover their capacity for good, All during Mercury’s evolution from a sleepy backwater to a small city, Finus (reporter, radio host, and obit writer) has aimed to have the last word on its inhabitants, from obsequious undertaker Parnell Grimes to Euple Scarbrough, local encyclopedia of useless knowledge, and Vish, a real oldtime medicine woman. But ever loyal to Birdie, Finus won’t rush to unravel the mystery of her husband’s untimely passing—not until Birdie herself has entered Mercury’s heaven.\n\nA gorgeous portrayal of lifelong friendship, restless passion, marital discord accommodation, aging and remembrance, death and afterlife, The Heaven of Mercury is inscribed in prose so eerily fine it is one of life’s true pleasures.\n\nEarly readers have compared Watson to William Faulkner, Flannery O’Connor, and Eudora Welty, and such distinguished ghosts surely grace this book. In this insightful book, written with dry wit and sharp insight, James C. Cobb explains how the South first came to be seen--and then came to see itself--as a region apart from the rest of America. As Cobb demonstrates, the legend of the aristocratic Cavalier origins of southern planter society was nurtured by both northern and southern writers, only to be challenged by abolitionist critics, black and white. After the Civil War, defeated and embittered southern whites incorporated the Cavalier myth into the cult of the &amp;quot;Lost Cause,&amp;quot; which supplied the emotional energy for their determined crusade to rejoin the Union on their own terms. After World War I, white writers like Ellen Glasgow, William Faulkner and other key figures of &amp;quot;Southern Renaissance&amp;quot; as well as their African American counterparts in the &amp;quot;Harlem Renaissance&amp;quot;--Cobb is the first to show the strong links between the two movements--challenged the New South creed by asking how the grandiose vision of the South\'s past could be reconciled with the dismal reality of its present. The Southern self-image underwent another sea change in the wake of the Civil Rights movement, when the end of white supremacy shook the old definition of the &amp;quot;Southern way of life&amp;quot;--but at the same time, African Americans began to examine their southern roots more openly and embrace their regional, as well as racial, identity. As the millennium turned, the South confronted a new identity crisis brought on by global homogenization: if Southern culture is everywhere, has the New South become the No South? Here then is a major work by one of America\'s finest Southern historians, a magisterial synthesis that combines rich scholarship with provocative new insights into what the South means to southerners
